# Chapter 104: Persistent (Chronic) Motor or Vocal Tic Disorder

# The Biological and Neurological Basis of Persistent (Chronic) Motor or Vocal Tic Disorder

## Introduction

Persistent (Chronic) Motor or Vocal Tic Disorder, a key entity within the spectrum of tic disorders, represents a complex neurodevelopmental condition characterized by the presence of one or more motor or vocal tics, but not both, for a duration exceeding one year (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). Unlike transient tic disorders, which are common in childhood and often resolve spontaneously (Wüthrich, 2013), persistent tic disorder signifies a chronic illness trajectory that can significantly impact an individual's quality of life (Mahmood & Otify, 2021; Malleson, 2001). The tics themselves are sudden, rapid, recurrent, nonrhythmic motor movements or vocalizations (정건, 2012). They are often described as involuntary, though many individuals experience a preceding premonitory urge—an uncomfortable sensory feeling that is relieved by the execution of the tic—suggesting a more complex interplay between involuntary and voluntary control mechanisms (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008).

From a biological perspective, persistent tic disorder is not a sign of a hidden psychological malady but a neuropsychiatric illness with demonstrable roots in brain function and genetics (Wüthrich, 2013). The manifestation of symptoms typically begins in childhood, usually between the ages of 5 and 7, and often follows a waxing and waning course, with periods of symptom exacerbation and remission (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008; Wüthrich, 2013). This chronicity and variability are hallmarks of many long-term illnesses, which can disrupt home, social, and work life over extended periods (Whittaker, 2004; Lewer & Robertson, 1987). Understanding the neurobiological underpinnings of this disorder is paramount for developing effective diagnostic and therapeutic strategies.

For decades, research has progressively moved away from psychogenic explanations towards a neurobiological model centered on dysfunction within specific brain circuits, particularly the cortico-striato-thalamo-cortical (CSTC) pathways (Sandberg, 2003). This model posits that tics arise from a failure to appropriately inhibit unwanted motor and vocal programs. The pathophysiology is thought to involve a complex interplay of neurotransmitter dysregulation, genetic predispositions, and environmental factors that influence brain development and plasticity (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). The study of this disorder provides a valuable window into the neural mechanisms of motor control, habit formation, and the suppression of unwanted actions. This chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of the current, evidence-based understanding of the biological and neurological basis of Persistent Motor or Vocal Tic Disorder, exploring the intricate web of systems from the molecular and genetic level to the large-scale neural networks that govern its expression. We will examine the roles of key neurotransmitter systems, the specific brain regions and circuits implicated, the growing body of neuroimaging evidence, and the genetic factors that confer susceptibility. Finally, we will consider the developmental context of the disorder and the biological rationale for current and emerging treatments.

## Neurobiological Systems

The prevailing neurobiological model of persistent tic disorder centers on abnormalities within the cortico-striato-thalamo-cortical (CSTC) circuits. These re-entrant loops are crucial for motor planning, execution, habit formation, and cognitive control (Sandberg, 2003). A failure within these circuits, particularly in the inhibitory pathways that suppress unwanted movements, is thought to be the core pathophysiological mechanism of tics (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). This dysfunction is not isolated to a single neurochemical or anatomical locus but reflects a systems-level problem involving multiple neurotransmitters and interconnected brain regions.

### Neurotransmitter Systems and Neuropharmacology

A wealth of evidence points to the central role of dopamine (DA) in the modulation of CSTC pathways and, consequently, in the pathophysiology of tics. The "dopamine hypothesis" has been the dominant model for decades, supported by several lines of evidence. First, dopamine-blocking agents (antipsychotics) are the most effective pharmacological treatments for suppressing tics, whereas dopamine-enhancing agents can induce or exacerbate them (Wüthrich, 2013). This suggests a state of dopaminergic hyperactivity or hypersensitivity within the striatum, a key node in the CSTC loops (Sandberg, 2003). This hyperactivity could result from excessive presynaptic DA release, increased density or sensitivity of postsynaptic DA receptors (particularly D2 receptors), or deficient DA reuptake. The management of many chronic neurological conditions often involves manipulating neurotransmitter systems to restore balance, whether it be for pain, seizures, or movement disorders (樊碧发 et al., 2014; Cendes, 2002).

While dopamine has received the most attention, it is clear that other neurotransmitter systems are also involved, creating a more complex picture of neurochemical imbalance.

**Serotonin (5-HT):** Serotonin systems, which originate in the brainstem raphe nuclei and project widely throughout the brain, have a crucial modulatory influence on dopaminergic pathways. There is evidence to suggest that serotonergic dysfunction may contribute to tic disorders, possibly by disinhibiting dopaminergic activity in the basal ganglia (Sandberg, 2003). The efficacy of selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors (SSRIs) in treating obsessive-compulsive disorder (OCD), a frequent comorbidity of tic disorders, further implicates serotonin in the broader neurobiology of these conditions (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008).

**GABA and Glutamate:** The striatum, the primary input nucleus of the basal ganglia, is composed mainly of GABAergic medium spiny neurons (MSNs). These neurons form the direct and indirect pathways that are essential for facilitating desired movements and inhibiting unwanted ones. A deficit in GABAergic inhibitory tone within these pathways could lead to the disinhibition of thalamo-cortical circuits, resulting in the unwanted motor output characteristic of tics (Sandberg, 2003). Conversely, glutamate is the primary excitatory neurotransmitter in the CSTC loops, driving activity from the cortex to the striatum. An imbalance between excitatory glutamatergic input and inhibitory GABAergic control within the striatum is a plausible mechanism for tic generation. The interplay between excitation and inhibition is a fundamental principle of neural function, and its disruption is implicated in a variety of neurological events, from seizures to spasms (Cendes, 2002; Aroudam et al., 2023).

**Other Systems:** Noradrenergic, cholinergic, and histaminergic systems also play modulatory roles in the CSTC circuits and may be implicated in tic disorders (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). For example, noradrenergic agents like clonidine and guanfacine, which act on α2-adrenergic autoreceptors to reduce norepinephrine release, have shown efficacy in treating tics, suggesting a role for noradrenergic hyperactivity (Wüthrich, 2013). The study of hypothalamic function in related conditions has also highlighted the complexity of neuromodulatory influences on behavior and motor control (Sandyk et al., 1987).

### Neural Circuits and Functional Connectivity

The CSTC loops are a set of parallel, largely segregated circuits that link cortical areas with the basal ganglia and thalamus. Traditionally, these have been divided into motor, oculomotor, associative/prefrontal, and limbic loops. The motor loop, originating in the supplementary motor area (SMA), premotor cortex, and primary motor cortex, is most directly implicated in tic generation (Sandberg, 2003).

The classic model posits that cortical signals project to the striatum (caudate and putamen). The striatum then projects via two main pathways to the output nuclei of the basal ganglia, the internal globus pallidus (GPi) and the substantia nigra pars reticulata (SNr). The "direct pathway" facilitates movement by inhibiting these output nuclei, which in turn disinhibits the thalamus, allowing it to drive cortical activity. The "indirect pathway" suppresses movement by ultimately exciting the output nuclei, which increases their tonic inhibition of the thalamus.

In tic disorders, this delicate balance is disrupted. A leading hypothesis is that phasic, inappropriate bursts of dopamine in the striatum excessively activate the direct pathway, leading to a transient disinhibition of the thalamus and the release of an unwanted motor program (a tic) (Sandberg, 2003). Alternatively, deficits in the tonic inhibitory control provided by the indirect pathway could lower the threshold for tic execution. This model of disinhibition is central to understanding not only tics but also other paroxysmal neurological events like hiccups or certain types of seizures (Aroudam et al., 2023; Cendes, 2002).

Beyond the motor loop, the limbic and associative loops are also critically involved. The limbic loop, which includes the ventral striatum (nucleus accumbens) and limbic cortices (e.g., anterior cingulate, orbitofrontal), is involved in motivation, emotion, and reward. The premonitory urges that precede tics are thought to originate from dysfunction within this limbic circuitry, possibly involving the insula and anterior cingulate cortex (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). The execution of the tic provides relief from this urge, creating a powerful negative reinforcement cycle that may contribute to the persistence of the disorder (Lewer & Robertson, 1987). Similarly, the associative loop, involving the dorsolateral prefrontal cortex and the caudate nucleus, is crucial for executive functions and cognitive control. Deficits in this circuit may contribute to the difficulty individuals with tic disorders experience in suppressing their tics.

## Genetic and Molecular Basis

Persistent tic disorder is a highly heritable neurodevelopmental condition (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). Family and twin studies have consistently demonstrated a strong genetic component, although the mode of inheritance is complex and does not follow a simple Mendelian pattern. The risk for tic disorders is significantly higher in first-degree relatives of affected individuals, and concordance rates are substantially higher for monozygotic twins than for dizygotic twins (Sandberg, 2003). This pattern is typical of many complex diseases where genetic predisposition interacts with other factors (Weary, 1971; Canale & Williams, 1992). The heritability is not just for a specific tic disorder but for a broader spectrum of related conditions, including Tourette's syndrome and obsessive-compulsive disorder (OCD), suggesting a shared genetic diathesis.

### Candidate Genes and Molecular Pathways

The search for specific susceptibility genes has been challenging, reflecting the genetic heterogeneity and polygenic nature of the disorder. For many years, research focused on genes involved in the dopaminergic system, given its central role in pathophysiology. Genes encoding dopamine receptors (e.g., *DRD2*, *DRD4*), the dopamine transporter (*DAT1/SLC6A3*), and enzymes involved in dopamine synthesis and metabolism have been extensively investigated, though results have often been inconsistent across studies (Sandberg, 2003).

More recently, advances in genetic technology, including genome-wide association studies (GWAS) and whole-exome sequencing, have identified novel candidate genes and pathways. One of the first genes to be implicated through linkage analysis was *SLITRK1* (Slit and Trk-like 1), a neuronal development gene, although mutations in this gene appear to be rare (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). Another significant finding was the identification of mutations in the *HDC* gene, which encodes L-histidine decarboxylase, the rate-limiting enzyme in histamine synthesis. This finding implicates the histaminergic system, which is known to modulate other neurotransmitter systems in the basal ganglia, and aligns with the observation that some antihistamines can affect tic severity (Sandberg, 2003).

Other genes implicated by recent large-scale studies point towards pathways involved in cell adhesion, synaptic function, and neurodevelopment. For example, genes like *CNTNAP2* and *NRXN1*, which are involved in synaptic cell adhesion, have been associated with a spectrum of neurodevelopmental disorders including tic disorders (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). The involvement of such genes underscores the view of tic disorders as a condition of aberrant neural circuit development and synaptic function. The phenotypic heterogeneity seen in other genetic conditions, where the same mutation can lead to varying symptom severity, is also a feature of tic disorders, suggesting the influence of modifier genes and environmental factors (Помарино et al., 2022).

### Cellular and Synaptic Mechanisms

At the cellular level, the pathophysiology of tic disorders is thought to involve altered synaptic plasticity and excitability within the CSTC circuits. The primary cellular components of the striatum are the two populations of medium spiny neurons (MSNs) that give rise to the direct (D1-receptor expressing) and indirect (D2-receptor expressing) pathways. The balance of activity between these two pathways is critical for motor control (Sandberg, 2003).

A leading hypothesis suggests that tics result from abnormal synaptic plasticity at the corticostriatal synapse—the connection between glutamatergic inputs from the cortex and the MSNs. Dopamine plays a critical role in modulating this plasticity. A state of hyperdopaminergia could lead to long-term potentiation (LTP) in the direct pathway and long-term depression (LTD) in the indirect pathway, biasing the circuit towards movement generation and disinhibition (Sandberg, 2003). This could effectively "stamp in" certain motor patterns, making them more likely to be expressed involuntarily as tics.

Furthermore, there is growing evidence for a role of striatal interneurons. These interneurons, which include cholinergic and various GABAergic subtypes, provide local modulation of MSN activity. For example, tonically active cholinergic interneurons can influence dopamine release and directly modulate MSN excitability. A disruption in the function of these interneurons could contribute to the overall imbalance of the striatal microcircuit, further promoting tic generation. The idea that local inflammation or autoimmune processes could disrupt these delicate cellular interactions has also been proposed, drawing parallels with other conditions where inflammation contributes to chronic neurological symptoms (Eisenstein & Taubenhaus, 1958; Kessel & Toubi, 2008).

## Brain Structure and Function

Neuroimaging has been instrumental in translating the theoretical CSTC model of tic disorders into a framework supported by anatomical and functional evidence. Both structural and functional imaging studies have revealed subtle but consistent differences in the brains of individuals with tic disorders compared to neurotypical controls.

### Structural Neuroimaging Findings

Structural Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) studies have primarily focused on measuring the volume of brain regions within the CSTC circuits. The most replicated finding is a reduction in the volume of the caudate nucleus and lenticular nucleus (putamen and globus pallidus) in children and adolescents with tic disorders (Sandberg, 2003). This finding is somewhat paradoxical, as it contrasts with the hyperactivity model of the basal ganglia. However, it is hypothesized that this volumetric reduction may reflect deficits in the development of the striatal matrix or abnormal synaptic pruning during a critical neurodevelopmental window (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). Interestingly, some longitudinal studies suggest that these volumetric differences may normalize or even reverse in adulthood, potentially correlating with the common clinical improvement of tics with age (Wüthrich, 2013). The use of MRI to confirm neurological origins of complex symptoms is a common approach across many disorders (Aroudam et al., 2023).

Diffusion Tensor Imaging (DTI), a technique that measures the directional diffusion of water to map white matter tracts, has provided further insights. Studies using DTI have found evidence of altered white matter integrity in tracts connecting cortical motor and sensory areas with the striatum and thalamus, suggesting that the connectivity between key nodes of the CSTC circuit may be atypical (Sandberg, 2003).

### Functional Neuroimaging Findings

Functional MRI (fMRI) and Positron Emission Tomography (PET) have allowed researchers to investigate brain activity patterns associated with tic disorders. PET studies have provided evidence supporting the dopamine hypothesis, with some studies showing increased density of dopamine transporters or altered D2 receptor binding in the striatum (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008).

fMRI studies have been crucial in identifying the neural correlates of both tic generation and suppression. Studies that capture brain activity during tic execution often show activation in a network including the sensorimotor cortex, supplementary motor area (SMA), thalamus, and basal ganglia, consistent with the motor loop's involvement (Sandberg, 2003). Crucially, activity in limbic regions like the anterior cingulate cortex and insula often precedes the tic, providing a neural correlate for the premonitory urge (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008).

Studies examining voluntary tic suppression have been equally informative. During successful suppression, increased activity is typically seen in prefrontal and parietal cortical regions, particularly the dorsolateral prefrontal cortex (DLPFC) and orbitofrontal cortex (OFC) (Sandberg, 2003). This suggests that tic suppression is an active cognitive process that requires engaging top-down executive control circuits to override the disinhibited motor commands emanating from the basal ganglia. The constant effort required for this suppression may contribute to the fatigue and attentional problems reported by many individuals with tic disorders (Kar, 2005). The fluctuating nature of brain activity and symptoms is a feature shared with other chronic neurological conditions, such as cluster headaches or Menière's disease (Takase, 2005; James & Thorp, 2004).

## Developmental Neurobiology

Persistent tic disorder is fundamentally a neurodevelopmental condition, with its onset, clinical course, and prognosis intrinsically linked to the processes of brain maturation (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). The disorder typically emerges during a specific developmental window and its expression changes dynamically across the lifespan.

### Onset, Course, and Critical Periods

The onset of tics almost invariably occurs in childhood, with a peak age of onset between 5 and 7 years (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008; Wüthrich, 2013). The emergence during this period is thought to be related to ongoing maturational processes within the CSTC circuits and their associated neurotransmitter systems. During middle childhood and pre-adolescence, the brain undergoes significant synaptic pruning and myelination, particularly in frontal-striatal pathways. It is hypothesized that genetic and environmental risk factors may disrupt these normal developmental trajectories, leading to the formation of the dysfunctional circuits that underlie tics (Sandberg, 2003). The initial symptoms are often simple motor tics like eye blinking or facial grimacing (Wüthrich, 2013).

The clinical course is classically characterized by a waxing and waning of tic severity and frequency (Robertson & Cavanna, 2008). Tic symptoms often peak in severity during the pre-teen or early adolescent years (ages 10-12) and then show significant improvement for a majority of individuals during late adolescence and early adulthood (Wüthrich, 2013). This typical trajectory suggests that ongoing brain development, particularly the maturation of prefrontal cortical control systems, may enable better compensatory control over the subcortical disinhibition driving the tics. However, for a subset of individuals, tics persist into adulthood with significant severity, a course that parallels other chronic conditions that begin in childhood and continue indefinitely (Lewer & Robertson, 1987; Malleson, 2001). The reasons for this heterogeneity in long-term outcomes are not fully understood but likely involve a combination of genetic loading, environmental stressors, and the presence of comorbidities.

### Neuroplasticity and Compensatory Mechanisms

The brain's capacity for plasticity is a key factor in the natural history of tic disorders. The spontaneous improvement seen in many adolescents is a testament to the brain's ability to develop compensatory mechanisms. As noted in functional imaging studies, successful tic suppression is associated with robust activation of prefrontal control networks (Sandberg, 2003). It is plausible that the maturation of these frontal lobe systems during adolescence enhances their ability to exert top-down inhibitory control over the hyperactive motor circuits in the basal ganglia.

This model has direct implications for treatment. Behavioral therapies such as the Comprehensive Behavioral Intervention for Tics (CBIT) are explicitly designed to leverage neuroplasticity. CBIT teaches individuals to become more aware of their premonitory urges and to perform a competing motor response that is physically incompatible with the tic. Through repeated practice, this process is thought to strengthen the cortical pathways that control the striatum, effectively "retraining" the brain to better inhibit the
